<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41438"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="High-quality de novo assembly of the Eucommia ulmoides haploid genome provides new insights into evolution and rubber biosynthesis"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Horticulture Research 2020 7:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-11-01"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="We report the acquisition of a high-quality haploid chromosome-scale genome assembly for the first time in a tree species, Eucommia ulmoides, which is known for its rubber biosynthesis and medicinal applications. The assembly was obtained by applying PacBio and Hi&#8211;C technologies to a haploid that we specifically generated. Compared to the initial genome release, this one has significantly improved assembly quality. The scaffold N50 (53.15&#8201;MB) increased 28-fold, and the repetitive sequence content (520&#8201;Mb) increased by 158.24&#8201;Mb, whereas the number of gaps decreased from 104,772 to 128. A total of 92.87% of the 26,001 predicted protein-coding genes identified with multiple strategies were anchored to the 17 chromosomes. A new whole-genome duplication event was superimposed on the earlier &#947; paleohexaploidization event, and the expansion of long terminal repeats contributed greatly to the evolution of the genome. The more primitive rubber biosynthesis of this species, as opposed to that in Hevea brasiliensis, relies on the methylerythritol-phosphate pathway rather than the mevalonate pathway to synthesize isoprenyl diphosphate, as the MEP pathway operates predominantly in trans-polyisoprene-containing leaves and central peels. Chlorogenic acid biosynthesis pathway enzymes were preferentially expressed in leaves rather than in bark. This assembly with higher sequence contiguity can foster not only studies on genome structure and evolution, gene mapping, epigenetic analysis and functional genomics but also efforts to improve E. ulmoides for industrial and medical uses through genetic engineering."/>

The assembly was obtained by applying PacBio and Hi&#8211;C technologies to a haploid that we specifically generated. This assembly with higher sequence contiguity can foster not only studies on genome structure and evolution, gene mapping, epigenetic analysis and functional genomics but also efforts to improve E. ulmoides for industrial and medical uses through genetic engineering."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>We report the acquisition of a high-quality haploid chromosome-scale genome assembly for the first time in a tree species, <i>Eucommia ulmoides</i>, which is known for its rubber biosynthesis and medicinal applications. The assembly was obtained by applying PacBio and HiâC technologies to a haploid that we specifically generated. Compared to the initial genome release, this one has significantly improved assembly quality. The scaffold N50 (53.15âMB) increased 28-fold, and the repetitive sequence content (520âMb) increased by 158.24âMb, whereas the number of gaps decreased from 104,772 to 128. A total of 92.87% of the 26,001 predicted protein-coding genes identified with multiple strategies were anchored to the 17 chromosomes. A new whole-genome duplication event was superimposed on the earlier Î³ paleohexaploidization event, and the expansion of long terminal repeats contributed greatly to the evolution of the genome. The more primitive rubber biosynthesis of this species, as opposed to that in <i>Hevea brasiliensis</i>, relies on the methylerythritol-phosphate pathway rather than the mevalonate pathway to synthesize isoprenyl diphosphate, as the MEP pathway operates predominantly in <i>trans</i>-polyisoprene-containing leaves and central peels. Chlorogenic acid biosynthesis pathway enzymes were preferentially expressed in leaves rather than in bark. This assembly with higher sequence contiguity can foster not only studies on genome structure and evolution, gene mapping, epigenetic analysis and functional genomics but also efforts to improve <i>E. ulmoides</i> for industrial and medical uses through genetic engineering.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Polyisoprene, a polymer of isoprene (C5H8), is the primary chemical constituent of natural rubber. Based on the chemical structure of the two isoprene isomers, natural rubber can be classified into <i>cis</i>-polyisoprene (CPI) and <i>trans</i>-polyisoprene (TPI)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Baboo, M., Dixit, M., Sharma, K. &amp; Saxena, N. S. Mechanical and thermal characterization of cis-polyisoprene and trans-polyisoprene blends. Polym. Bull. 66, 661â672 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d20745e586">1</a></sup>. Currently, the Brazilian rubber tree is the only commercial source of natural CPI<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Mooibroek, H. &amp; Cornish, K. Alternative sources of natural rubber. Appl. Microbiol. Biotechnol. 53, 355â365 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d20745e590">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Van Beilen, J. B. &amp; Poirier, Y. Establishment of new crops for the production of natural rubber. Trends Biotechnol. 25, 522â529 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d20745e593">3</a></sup>. This tropical tree species faces several challenges, however, including a narrow range of suitable habitats, densely planted areas for latex tapping, and threats from serious diseases such as blight and insect pests<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Lieberei, R. South American leaf blight of the rubber tree (Hevea spp.): new steps in plant domestication using physiological features and molecular markers. Ann. Bot. 100, 1125â1142 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d20745e597">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Tang, C. et al. The rubber tree genome reveals new insights into rubber production and species adaptation. Nat. Plants 2, 16073 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d20745e600">5</a></sup>. For these reasons, the shortage of natural rubber caused by an increasing global demand has led both governments and industrial enterprises to look for substitutes for this essential material to synthesize various rubber products.</p><p><i>Eucommia ulmoides</i> is a tertiary relic perennial tree of high value. It is an important temperate economic tree species that is capable of producing not only wood but also valuable raw biomaterials for extracting rubber and the active ingredients of a traditional Chinese medicine called Duzhong<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bamba, T., Fukusaki, E., Nakazawa, Y. &amp; Kobayashi, A. In-situ chemical analyses of trans-polyisoprene by histochemical staining and Fourier transform infrared microspectroscopy in a rubber-producing plant, Eucommia ulmoides Oliver. Planta 215, 934â939 (2002)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d20745e609">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Du, H. Y., Hu, W. Z. &amp; Yu, R. The Report on Development of Chinaâs Eucommia Rubber Resources and Industry (2014â2015) (Social Sciences Academic Press, 2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d20745e612">7</a></sup>. Unlike the Brazilian rubber tree, <i>E. ulmoides</i> is a well-known tree species for producing <i>trans</i>-rubber, which has unique characteristics such as high hardness, a low thermal expansion/contraction coefficient, good insulation, and resistance to acid and alkaline conditions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kent, E. G. &amp; Swinney, F. B. Properties and applications of trans-1,4-polyisoprene. Ind. Eng. Chem. Prod. Res. Dev. 5, 134â138 (1966)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d20745e622">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Enoki, M., Doi, Y. &amp; Iwata, T. Oxidative degradation of cis- and trans-1,4-polyisoprenes and vulcanized natural rubber with enzyme-mediator systems. Biomacromolecules 4, 314â320 (2003)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d20745e622_1">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Rose, K. &amp; Steinbuchel, A. Biodegradation of natural rubber and related compounds: recent insights into a hardly understood catabolic capability of microorganisms. Appl. Environ. Microbiol. 71, 2803â2812 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d20745e625">10</a></sup>. For this reason, it can be used to manufacture cable insulation and golf balls<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Schlesinger, W. &amp; Leeper, H. M. Chicle - cis- and trans-polyisoprenes from a single plant species. Ind. Eng. Chem. 43, 398â403 (1951)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d20745e629">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Roth, W. B., Carr, M. E., Davis, E. A. &amp; Bagby, M. O. New sources of gutta-percha in Garrya flavescens and G. wrightii. Phytochemistry 24, 183â184 (1985)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d20745e632">12</a></sup>. Moreover, since blended rubber of CPI and TPI has some augmented mechanical properties and a different thermal conductivity and diffusivity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Baboo, M., Dixit, M., Sharma, K. &amp; Saxena, N. S. Mechanical and thermal characterization of cis-polyisoprene and trans-polyisoprene blends. Polym. Bull. 66, 661â672 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d20745e637">1</a></sup> compared to those of rubber with CPI only, TPI has been made into <i>cis</i>-rubber by vulcanization to achieve improved elasticity, resilience, tensile strength, viscosity, hardness and weather resistance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Yan, R. F. Prospects and research progress on Eucommia ulmoides gum. Prog. Chem. 7, 65â71 (1995)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d20745e644">13</a></sup>.</p><p>As previously reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bamba, T., Fukusaki, E., Nakazawa, Y. &amp; Kobayashi, A. In-situ chemical analyses of trans-polyisoprene by histochemical staining and Fourier transform infrared microspectroscopy in a rubber-producing plant, Eucommia ulmoides Oliver. Planta 215, 934â939 (2002)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d20745e651">6</a></sup>, TPI is especially enriched in the leaves, bark, and peels of <i>E. ulmoides</i> trees. For this reason, <i>E. ulmoides</i> is considered an ideal alternative or supplementary tree species for producing the raw materials of natural rubber<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Nakazawa, Y. et al. Production of Eucommia-rubber from Eucommia ulmoides Oliv (Hardy Rubber Tree). Plant Biotechnol. 26, 71â79 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d20745e661">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Wuyun, T. N. et al. The hardy rubber tree genome provides insights into the evolution of polyisoprene biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 11, 429â442 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d20745e664">15</a></sup>. Moreover, <i>E. ulmoides</i> can grow in many countries located in the northern temperate zone, where Brazilian rubber trees cannot be domesticated. In addition to its value in producing rubber, <i>E. ulmoides</i> is an important source of some active ingredients in traditional Chinese medicine. Its leaves and bark are especially rich in chlorogenic acid (CGA), an antioxidant that is known to slow the release of glucose into the bloodstream after a meal<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Ong, K. W., Hsu, A. &amp; Tan, B. K. Chlorogenic acid stimulates glucose transport in skeletal muscle via AMPK activation: a contributor to the beneficial effects of coffee on diabetes. PLoS ONE 7, e32718 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d20745e675">16</a></sup> and reduce blood pressure<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Onakpoya, I. J., Spencer, E. A., Thompson, M. J. &amp; Heneghan, C. J. The effect of chlorogenic acid on blood pressure: a systematic review and meta-analysis of randomized clinical trials. J. Hum. Hypertens. 29, 77â81 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d20745e679">17</a></sup>. In addition, it contains aucubin, rutin, quercetin and other unknown ingredients that may have great value for biopharmaceuticals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Li, F. D. &amp; Du, H. Y. Eucommia ulmoides (China Press of Traditional Chinese Medicine, 2001)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d20745e683">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Kwan, C. Y., Chen, C. X., Deyama, T. &amp; Nishibe, S. Endothelium-dependent vasorelaxant effects of the aqueous extracts of the Eucommia ulmoides Oliv. leaf and bark: implications on their antihypertensive action. Vasc. Pharmacol. 40, 229â235 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d20745e686">19</a></sup>. Therefore, a high-quality genome is indispensable for genetic improvements related to <i>E. ulmoides</i> rubber productivity and medicinal uses in addition to wood.</p><p>In 2018, a draft of the <i>E. ulmoides</i> genome (v1.0) was released<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Wuyun, T. N. et al. The hardy rubber tree genome provides insights into the evolution of polyisoprene biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 11, 429â442 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d20745e699">15</a></sup>. Due to high heterozygosity (0.8â1%) and a large percentage of repetitive sequences (greater than 60%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Wuyun, T. N. et al. The hardy rubber tree genome provides insights into the evolution of polyisoprene biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 11, 429â442 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d20745e703">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Wuyun, T. N., Du, H. Y., Liu, H. M., Wang, L. &amp; Bao, W. Q. Important Research Progress in Genome Sequencing of Eucommia ulmoides (2014-2015) (Social Sciences Academic Press, 2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d20745e706">20</a></sup>, genome assembly from diploid sequences can easily incorporate chromosomal fragments from two homologous chromosomes in alternation and increase errors at the connections of contigs/scaffolds, thereby resulting in inaccurate assemblies with poor adjacency and increased linkage and base-error rates. To improve the quality of the <i>E. ulmoides</i> genome sequence, a haploid <i>E. ulmoides</i> (2<i>n</i>â=â<i>x</i>â=â17) individual was generated through parthenogenesis and used for genome sequencing. The genome (v2.0) was acquired by assembling PacBio long reads and anchoring scaffolds based on information from HiâC interaction confirmation. Using this high-quality genome, additional transcriptomic analyses of different tissues were undertaken to investigate rubber and CGA biosynthesis pathways in <i>E. ulmoides</i>. The genome sequence and gene annotation that we generated from the haploid will be instrumental for all studies on genome structure and evolution, gene linkage and mapping, epigenetic and chromatin-based analyses, and functional genomics as well as genetic engineering of <i>E. ulmoides</i> for industrial and medical uses.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Induction of haploidy by high-temperature exposure</h3><p>To induce haploidy through parthenogenesis, female flower buds of <i>E. ulmoides</i> in the developmental stage of embryo sac formation were treated with different combinations of high temperatures and treatment times, and untreated female flower buds were used as controls. A total of 2675 seeds were harvested after the treatments, and 835 plants were eventually obtained after sowing seeds and transplanting seedlings. Using flow cytometry and analysis of anatomical sections of stem tips, three authentic <i>Eucommia</i> haploid plants were obtained exclusively from the treated group (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#Fig1">1aâd</a>). Of these, one haploid was obtained from continuous treatment at 48âÂ°C for 6âh, while the other two haploid plants were obtained from continuous treatment at 54âÂ°C for 4âh.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Karyotypic analysis of haploid plants generated through parthenogenesis under high-temperature treatments (48âÂ°C for 6âh and 54âÂ°C for 4âh) in E. ulmoides."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Karyotypic analysis of haploid plants generated through parthenogenesis under high-temperature treatments (48âÂ°C for 6âh and 54âÂ°C for 4âh) in <i>E. ulmoides</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00406-w/MediaObjects/41438_2020_406_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00406-w/MediaObjects/41438_2020_406_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="218" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b> Somatic chromosome number of the haploids (2<i>n</i>â=â<i>x</i>â=â17). <b>b</b> Ploidy levels obtained from 3-week-old first leaf samples from haploid plants by flow cytometric analysis. <b>c</b> Ploidy levels obtained from 3-week-old first leaf samples from a mixture of haploid and diploid plants by flow cytometric analysis. <b>d</b> A haploid plant (left) and diploid plant (right) of <i>E. ulmoides</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>We conducted de novo sequencing using the PacBio Sequel platform, which yielded a total of 97.26âGb of raw data, representing a 95Ã coverage depth of the 1.02âGb genome as estimated by 17-Kmer evaluation (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM4">1</a> and Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM1">S1</a> and S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM3">2</a>). The PacBio long reads were corrected and assembled into 564 contigs using FALCON and Quiver<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Nonhybrid, finished microbial genome assemblies from long-read SMRT sequencing data. Nat. Methods 10, 563â569 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d20745e811">21</a></sup>, with a contig N50 of 13.16âMb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#Tab1">1</a> and Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM4">2</a>), which is 632 times longer than the contig N50 of the v1.0 genome. The total length of these contigs is 947.84âMb of genome sequence (Tables S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM4">2</a> and S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM4">3</a>), accounting for 92.93% of the estimated genome size. In addition, 133.74âGb of high-quality HiâC data with ~130Ã coverage was generated by the Illumina NovaSeq platform (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM4">1</a>). Valid high-quality HiâC data were aligned with PacBio contigs using Burrows-Wheeler Aligner (BWA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754â1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d20745e831">22</a></sup>, and duplicates were removed with SAMTOOLS rmdup<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078â2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d20745e835">23</a></sup> before LACHESIS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119â1125 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d20745e840">24</a></sup> was employed to assemble them into scaffolds based on linkage information and restriction enzyme sites. Finally, 145 long scaffolds were anchored to 17 linkage groups and further formed pseudochromosomes, accounting for 94.94% of the assembly (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM4">4</a>). The genome (v2.0) finally acquired contains a total of 501 scaffolds with a scaffold N50 of 53.15âMb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#Tab1">1</a> and Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM4">3</a>), which is 28-fold longer than that in v1.0. Compared to the 104,772 gaps between adjacent scaffolds in v1.0, the number of gaps in v2.0 is only 128. Core Eukaryotic Genes Mapping Approach (CEGMA) evaluation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061â1067 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d20745e853">25</a></sup> was undertaken and showed that the newly assembled genome covered 230 (92.74%) of the 248 core eukaryotic genes (CEGs) completely and 8 (3.23%) of them partially, and the number of undetected CEGs was therefore 10 (4.04%) (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM4">5</a>). Genome completeness was assessed using Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCOs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d20745e861">26</a></sup>, which revealed complete genes (91.3%) and partial genes (1.7%) (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM4">6</a>). To further evaluate the assembly schemes, high-quality reads from a small-fragment library (350âbp) were aligned to the assembled genome. The results showed that the alignment rate was 99.18%, and the genome coverage was 99.86% (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM4">7</a>). A high degree of consistency between the HiâC and PacBio results was also illustrated (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM3">3</a>). These results provide evidence that the new <i>E. ulmoides</i> genome assembled from the haploid is of high quality.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics for the <i>Eucommia</i> genome and gene annotation.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Gene content and annotation</h3><p>Multiple strategies, including de novo prediction, homology-based methods, and transcript alignment, were employed for gene identification and annotation. Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Keller, O., Kollmar, M., Stanke, M. &amp; Waack, S. A novel hybrid gene prediction method employing protein multiple sequence alignments. Bioinformatics 27, 757â763 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1221">27</a></sup>, GlimmerHMM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Majoros, W. H., Pertea, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics 20, 2878â2879 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1225">28</a></sup> and SNAP (<a href="http://homepage.mac.com/iankorf/">http://homepage.mac.com/iankorf/</a>) software were used for de novo gene prediction, whereas BLAST<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Altschul, S. F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E. W. &amp; Lipman, D. J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403â410 (1990)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1236">29</a></sup> and Genewise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988â995 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1240">30</a></sup> were utilized to identify homologous open reading frames (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM4">8</a>). RNA-seq data derived from 12 different tissue types were assembled with Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644â652 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1248">31</a></sup>, and the assembled sequences were aligned against the <i>E. ulmoides</i> genome by PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654â5666 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1255">32</a></sup>. A total of 26,001 protein-coding genes, compared to 26,723 in v1.0, were predicted. The average length of the predicted genes was 6,138.21âbp, the average coding sequence (CDS) length was 1,108.80âbp, the average number of exons contained in each gene was 4.85, the average exon length was 228.56âbp, and the average intron length was 1,305.93âbp (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM4">9</a> and Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM3">4</a>). In total, 98.8% of the 26,001 predicted genes had homologs in at least one of the six functional protein databases aligned (Figs. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM3">5</a>, S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM3">6</a> and Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM4">10</a>), and 24,148 genes could be localized to the chromosome, accounting for approximately 92.87% of the total predicted genes. The GC content, repeat density and gene density for each chromosome were plotted with Circos<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Krzywinski, M. et al. Circos: an information aesthetic for comparative genomics. Genome Res. 19, 1639â1645 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1275">33</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#Fig2">2</a>). Many noncoding RNAs, including 2099 rRNAs, 825 tRNAs, 1032 miRNAs, and 875 snRNAs, were identified and are shown in Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM4">11</a>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Overview of the E. ulmoides genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Overview of the <i>E. ulmoides</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00406-w/MediaObjects/41438_2020_406_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00406-w/MediaObjects/41438_2020_406_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="677" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> Lengths of pseudochromosomes (0.5âMb window size (WS)); <b>b</b> gene density (0.3âMb WS); <b>c</b> repeat density (1âMb WS); <b>d</b> GC content (1âMb WS). The colored lines in the center show links between syntenic blocks of at least five genes</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The use of haploid <i>E. ulmoides</i> and PacBio data greatly facilitated the acquisition of repetitive sequences. A total of 592.43âMb of repeat sequences, accounting for 62.50% of the genome length, were identified in the <i>E. ulmoides</i> v2.0 genome (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#Tab1">1</a> and Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM4">12</a>). Combined homology-based and de novo approaches were used to identify transposable elements (TEs); long terminal repeats (LTRs), which are the main TE family in <i>E. ulmoides</i>, had a size of 520âMb, accounting for 50.33% of the assembled genome. This was much longer than the 361.76âMb of LTRs accounting for only 30.63% of the v1.0. genome. The copia- and gypsy-like LTRs covered 179.53âMb and 267.69âMb, respectively, accounting for 18.94% and 28.24% of the assembled genome. Only 3% of repeats in the v2.0 genome could not be classified into any known category (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM4">13</a>). These results indicate that the combination of a haploid genome and PacBio sequencing technology significantly improved the construction of repeat regions. Among the 8,264 intact LTR retrotransposons (LTR-RTs), 5254 and 2980 were gypsy-like and copia-like LTRs, respectively (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM4">14</a>). The estimated times for the intact LTR-RT insertion events indicate that the LTRs, copia-like LTRs and gypsy-like LTRs started to expand rapidly ~7.2 million years ago (Mya) and peaked ~5.2, 5.0 and 3.6âMya, respectively. This time period falls within the Pliocene epoch (5.33â2.58âMya) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#Fig3">3a</a>). The expansion of the copia type occurred before that of the gypsy type, causing intact LTR-RTs of the gypsy type to outnumber those of the copia type (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#Fig3">3a</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Evolution and synteny of the E. ulmoides genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Evolution and synteny of the <i>E. ulmoides</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00406-w/MediaObjects/41438_2020_406_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00406-w/MediaObjects/41438_2020_406_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1102" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> The insertion times for intact LTR-RTs in the <i>E. ulmoides</i> genome. The insertion times for LTR-RTs were calculated by the formula <i>T</i>â=â<i>K</i>/2r. T insertion time; r synonymous mutations/site/Mya; K the divergence between the two LTRs. A substitution rate of 8.25âÃâ10<sup>â9</sup> per site per year was used to calculate the insertion times. <b>b</b> Venn diagram of shared orthologs among the five species. Each number represents a gene family number. <b>c</b> Phylogenetic tree of 12 species based on orthologs of single-gene families. The number at the root (28,004) represents the number of gene families related to the common ancestor. The value above each branch denotes the number of gene families gained/lost during each round of genome duplication after diversification from the common ancestor. The red number below each branch denotes the speculated divergence time of each node. Bootstrap values for all nodes are above 50%. <b>d</b> Density distributions of Ks for paralogous genes. The peak values are shown in insets for <i>E. ulmoides</i> and <i>C. canephora</i>. <b>e</b> Density distributions of Ks for paralogous genes. The peak values are shown in insets for <i>E. ulmoides</i> and <i>S. lycopersicum</i>. <b>f</b> Schematic representation of syntenic genes among <i>E. ulmoides</i>, <i>V. vinifera</i> and <i>C. canephora</i>. Gray lines in the background indicate the collinear blocks of at least twenty genes within the <i>E. ulmoides</i> genome and other plant genomes, while the red lines highlight the syntenic gene pairs</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Evolution of the <i>E. ulmoides</i> genome</h3><p>Orthologous clustering of the predicted <i>E. ulmoides</i> proteins with those from 11 other representative species showed that 20,219 of the <i>E. ulmoides</i> protein-coding genes belong to 13,494 gene families with an average of 1.50 genes per family; 538 of these gene families are unique to <i>E. ulmoides</i> (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM4">15</a> and Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM3">7</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. Jr. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1458">34</a></sup>. KEGG analysis revealed that <i>E. ulmoides</i>-specific genes are enriched in several pathways, including environmental adaptation, amino acid metabolism, glycan biosynthesis, and lipid metabolism (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM5">S16</a>). Evolutionary analysis of gene families in <i>E. ulmoides</i>, together with those from 11 other plant species, was undertaken with CAFÃ (v 2.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="De Bie, T., Cristianini, N., Demuth, J. P. &amp; Hahn, M. W. CAFE: a computational tool for the study of gene family evolution. Bioinformatics 22, 1269â1271 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1471">35</a></sup>. The results showed that 74 gene families expanded, while 131 gene families contracted (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#Fig3">3c</a>). Comparative analysis of the protein-coding gene families of <i>E. ulmoides</i> and those in other closely related asterid species, including <i>Catharanthus roseus</i>, <i>Solanum tuberosum, Coffea arabica</i> and <i>Olea europaea</i>, revealed that 8836 of the 20,981 gene families were shared by these five species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#Fig3">3b</a>). The families that have expanded significantly are those that are mainly involved in cyanoamino acid metabolism, phenylpropionic acid biosynthesis, starch and sucrose metabolism, and glutathione metabolism (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM6">17</a>). This finding suggests that the expanded and unique gene families might be closely associated and have evolved to enhance environmental adaptability and to protect against herbivores and plant pathogens by increasing the production of high secondary metabolite contents.</p><p>There has been some controversy regarding classifying <i>E. ulmoides</i> into the lamiid lineage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Wuyun, T. N. et al. The hardy rubber tree genome provides insights into the evolution of polyisoprene biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 11, 429â442 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1503">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Liu, Z. H. Floral Organogenesis and Phylogenetic Analysis in Eucommia ulmoides Oilv (Peking University, 2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1506">36</a></sup>. To determine its evolutionary status, a phylogenetic tree was constructed based on 133 single-copy gene families shared by <i>E. ulmoides</i> and 11 other species. In addition, their divergence time was estimated (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#Fig3">3c</a>). The results indicate that <i>E. ulmoides</i> and four plant species from different categories of lamiids, including <i>C. roseus</i>, <i>S. tuberosum</i>, <i>C. arabica</i>, and <i>O. europaea</i>, are on the sister branches located in the basal group of the lamiid lineage. This result also supports the recent divergence of the campanulids <i>Daucus carota</i> and <i>Taraxacum kok-saghyz</i> and the recent divergence of Ericales (<i>Actinidia chinensis</i>) and Cornales (<i>Camptotheca acuminata</i>), which are sister groups located at the base of asterids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Zhang, N., Zeng, L., Shan, H. &amp; Ma, H. Highly conserved low-copy nuclear genes as effective markers for phylogenetic analyses in angiosperms. N. Phytol. 195, 923â937 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1545">37</a></sup>. The results of the phylogenetic tree are consistent with the systematic classification of the Angiosperm Phylogeny Group (APG IV, 2016). After calibrating the dates with those in the TimeTree database (<a href="http://www.timetree.org/">http://www.timetree.org/</a>), it is estimated that <i>E. ulmoides</i> diverged from campanulids ~100.5âMya.</p><p>Whole-genome duplication (WGD) or polyploidization has occurred in the evolutionary history of most plant species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Van de Peer, Y., Mizrachi, E. &amp; Marchal, K. The evolutionary significance of polyploidy. Nat. Rev. Genet. 18, 411â424 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1562">38</a></sup>. The distribution of synonymous substitutions (Ks) and 4-fold synonymous third-codon transversions (4DTv) between duplicated genes was used to extrapolate WGD events. The results show that the <i>E. ulmoides</i> genome has two peaks in the Ks (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#Fig3">3d, e</a>) and 4DTv distributions (Eulm_Eulm) (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM3">8a, b</a>), indicating that <i>E. ulmoides</i> may have undergone two WGD events. This is inconsistent with the results for the v1.0 genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Wuyun, T. N. et al. The hardy rubber tree genome provides insights into the evolution of polyisoprene biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 11, 429â442 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1579">15</a></sup>, where the peaks of Î³ duplication events in the Ks distribution analysis of <i>D. carota, Solanum lycopersicum</i>, and <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> were not conspicuous, presumably because less stringency was used in their analysis. Given that <i>Coffea canephora</i> and <i>Vitis vinifera</i> have only undergone an earlier Î³ paleohexaploidization event shared by all eudicots, the Î³ duplication events of <i>E. ulmoides</i> and <i>C. canephora</i> were very close to each other and resembled those in the <i>C. canephora</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Iorizzo, M. et al. A high-quality carrot genome assembly provides new insights into carotenoid accumulation and asterid genome evolution. Nat. Genet. 48, 657â666 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1605">39</a></sup>. Collinearity analysis of <i>E. ulmoides</i>, <i>C. canephora</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i> with JCVI<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Tang, H., Krishnakumar, V. &amp; Li, J. jcvi: JCVI utility libraries (Version v0.5.7). Zenodo (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1619">40</a></sup> revealed a 2:1 synteny pattern between either <i>E. ulmoides</i> and <i>C. canephora</i> or <i>E. ulmoides</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i> (Figs. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM3">9</a> and S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM3">10</a>). QUOTAÂ­ALIGN was then used to draw collinear dot plots to further validate the additional WGD upon Î³ paleohexaploidization (Figs. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM3">11</a> and S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM3">12</a>). As shown in Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM4">18</a>, when the ratio of <i>E. ulmoides</i> to either coffee or grape was 1:1, <i>E. ulmoides</i> coverage was only 65.6% and 61.5% compared to 92.5% and 94%, respectively, in <i>C. canephora</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i>. When the ratios of <i>E. ulmoides</i> to <i>C. canephora</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i> were both 2:1, <i>E. ulmoides</i> coverage increased to 96.2% and 97%, respectively; thereafter, the coverage of <i>E. ulmoides</i> remained unchanged (&lt;Â±0.5%), even when the ratio increased to a high value, such as 8:1. All of these results support that <i>E. ulmoides</i> underwent another WGD event after the earlier Î³ paleohexaploidization event shared by all eudicots (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#Fig3">3dâf</a> and Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM4">18</a>). Based on the repeated gene pairs in the genome, these two WGDs of <i>E. ulmoides</i> occurred 69.7 Mya and between 109.1 and 121.1âMya, respectively (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM4">19</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Metabolic gene clusters and rubber biosynthesis pathways</h3><p>Plant genes involved in secondary metabolic pathways are sometimes concentrated in specific genomic regions, resulting in biosynthesis gene clusters (BGCs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="De Bie, T., Cristianini, N., Demuth, J. P. &amp; Hahn, M. W. CAFE: a computational tool for the study of gene family evolution. Bioinformatics 22, 1269â1271 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1704">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chae, L., Kim, T., Nilo-Poyanco, R. &amp; Rhee, S. Y. Genomic signatures of specialized metabolism in plants. Science 344, 510â513 (2014)." href="#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1707">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Nutzmann, H. W., Huang, A. &amp; Osbourn, A. Plant metabolic clustersâfrom genetics to genomics. N. Phytol. 211, 771â789 (2016)." href="#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1707_1">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Kautsar, S. A., Suarez Duran, H. G., Blin, K., Osbourn, A. &amp; Medema, M. H. plantiSMASH: automated identification, annotation and expression analysis of plant biosynthetic gene clusters. Nucleic Acids Res. 45, W55âW63 (2017). Nucleic Acids Research." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1710">43</a></sup>. Using plantiSMASH<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Nutzmann, H. W. &amp; Osbourn, A. Gene clustering in plant specialized metabolism. Curr. Opin. Biotechnol. 26, 91â99 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1714">44</a></sup>, we identified 26 BGCs that are associated with various secondary metabolic pathways in <i>E. ulmoides</i> (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM7">20</a> and File S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM1">1</a>). Among these 26 BGCs are 9 saccharide-related BGCs, 6 terpene-related BGCs, 4 alkaloid-related BGCs, 1 polyketide-alkaloid-related BGC, 1 saccharide-terpene-related BGC, and 1 lignin-polyketide-related BGC. The other four putative BGCs could not be assigned to a specific secondary metabolic pathway. The sizes of the BGCs identified vary from 32.01 to 2191.17âkb, which usually contain 3â5 core protein domains related to secondary metabolism. These tightly linked BCGs may facilitate cosegregation of coadaptive variation and limit unfit recombinant BCGs, resulting in more stable secondary metabolism.</p><p><i>Trans</i>-polyisoprene (TPI) is known to be synthesized from isoprenyl diphosphate (IPP)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Bamba, T. et al. Contribution of mevalonate and methylerythritol phosphate pathways to polyisoprenoid biosynthesis in the rubber-producing plant Eucommia ulmoides Oliver. Z. Naturforsch. C. J. Biosci. 65, 363â372 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1732">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Suzuki, N. et al. Construction and analysis of EST libraries of the trans-polyisoprene producing plant, Eucommia ulmoides Oliver. Planta 236, 1405â1417 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1735">46</a></sup> through two pathways, namely, the mevalonate (MVA) pathway in the cytoplasm and the methylerythritol-phosphate (MEP) pathway in plastids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Rohmer, M. The discovery of a mevalonate-independent pathway for isoprenoid biosynthesis in bacteria, algae and higher plants. Nat. Prod. Rep. 16, 565â574 (1999)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1739">47</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Kuzuyama, T. Mevalonate and nonmevalonate pathways for the biosynthesis of isoprene units. Biosci. Biotechnol. Biochem. 66, 1619â1627 (2002)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1742">48</a></sup>. A total of 47 candidate genes involved in the TPI biosynthesis pathway were identified in the genome (v2.0), including 13 genes involved in 6 reactions of the MVA pathway; 11 genes involved in 7 reactions of the MEP pathway; 12 genes involved in initial reactions for producing initiators or precursors, which include geranyl diphosphate synthases (GPSs), geranylgeranyl diphosphateâsynthases (GGPSs) and farnesyl diphosphate synthases (FPSs)); and 11 genes for ârubber elongationâ on TPI particles (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM8">21</a> and Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM3">13</a>).</p><p>Earlier studies showed that in addition to xylem and seeds, <i>E. ulmoides</i> leaves and peels contain a certain proportion of high-molecular-weight TPI<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Bamba, T. et al. Contribution of mevalonate and methylerythritol phosphate pathways to polyisoprenoid biosynthesis in the rubber-producing plant Eucommia ulmoides Oliver. Z. Naturforsch. C. J. Biosci. 65, 363â372 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1758">45</a></sup>. The TPI content is rich in the central peels but relatively poor in the outer peel<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Cui, Y., Wang, M. &amp; Sun, K. Morphological study of gutta containing cells in Eucommia ulmoides Oliv. Chin. Bull. Bot. 16, 439â443 (1999)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1762">49</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Du, H. Y., Du, L. Y. &amp; Li, F. D. Dynamic of gutta-percha formation and accumulation in samara of Eucommia ulmoides. For. Res. 17, 185â191 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1765">50</a></sup>. Differential expression analysis of genes in different tissues revealed that for the MEP pathway in <i>E. ulmoides</i>, at least one and sometimes two genes that encode enzymes to catalyze each step showed predominant expression in both TPI-containing leaves and central peels, indicating that the synthesis of IPP via the MEP pathway was in operation in both leaves and central peels. Although some MVA pathway genes were also expressed at relatively high levels, there was a lack of TPI-containing tissues in which all genes along the pathway were consistently âswitched onâ. Overall, MEP pathway genes had the highest or moderate to high expression levels, whereas MVA pathway genes had low to moderate expression levels (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM8">21</a> and Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#Fig4">4</a> and Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM3">1</a>). It should be noted that prior to this study, we conducted a pilot experiment to examine the differential expression of pathway genes. Differential expression patterns in the pilot experiment were largely the same as those observed in the present study (see Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM8">21</a> and Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM3">14</a>). These results suggest that IPP may be primarily and stably synthesized through MEP in the <i>E. ulmoides</i> trees used in our study.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="The E. ulmoides rubber biosynthesis pathway and expression profiles of genes involved in the pathway."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: The <i>E. ulmoides</i> rubber biosynthesis pathway and expression profiles of genes involved in the pathway.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00406-w/MediaObjects/41438_2020_406_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00406-w/MediaObjects/41438_2020_406_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="582" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>The expression level is presented by log2-transformed fragments mapped per kilobase of transcript length per million total mapped reads (log2-FPKM). ACAT acetyl-coenzyme A (CoA) C-acetyltransferase; HMGS hydroxymethylglutaryl-CoA synthase; HMGR hydroxymethylglutaryl-CoA reductase; MVK mevalonate kinase; PMK 5-phosphomevalonate kinase; MPD mevalonate pyrophosphate decarboxylase; DXS 1-deoxy-d-xylulose 5-phosphate synthase; DXR 1-deoxy-d-xylulose 5-phosphate reductoisomerase; MCT 2-C-methyl-d-erythritol 4-phosphate cytidylyltransferase; CMK 4-(cytidine 5â²-diphospho)-2-C-methyl-d-erythritol kinase; MDS 2-C-methyl-d-erythritol 2,4-cyclodiphosphate synthase; HDS 4-hydroxy-3-methylbut-2-enyl diphosphate synthase; HDR 4-hydroxy-3-methylbut-2-enyl diphosphate reductase; IDI isopentenyl diphosphate isomerase; GPS geranyl diphosphate synthase; FPS farnesyl diphosphate synthase; GGPS, geranylgeranyl diphosphate synthase; SRPP small rubber particle protein; Acetyl-CoA acetyl coenzyme-A; Acetoacetyl-CoA, 3-acetoacetyl-CoA; HMG-CoA, 3-hydroxy-3-methylglutaryl-CoA; MVA mevalonate; MVA-5P mevalonate-5-phosphate; MVA-5PP mevalonate-5-diphosphate; GA-3-P, glyceraldehyde 3-phosphate; DXP, 1-deoxy-d-xylulose 5-phosphate; MEP 2-C-methyl-d-erythritol 4-phosphate; CME 4-(cytidine 5â²-diphospho)-2-C-methyl-d-erythritol; PCME 2-phospho-4-(cytidine 5â²-diphospho)-2-C-methyl-d-erythritol; CMEC 2-C-methyl-d-erythritol 2,4-cyclodiphosphate; HMED 4-hydroxy-3-methylbut-2-enyl diphosphate; IPP isopentenyl diphosphate; DMAPP, dimethylallyl diphosphate; GPP geranyl diphosphate; FPP farnesyl diphosphate; GGPP, geranylgeranyl diphosphate. LF leaf; CP central peel; PE peel edge; XM xylem; SD seed</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>All candidate genes involved in <i>trans</i>-IPP synthesis were obtained through homologous gene comparison, and the results are shown in Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM8">21</a>. Three GPS, 7 GGPS and 4 FPS genes were identified in this <i>E. ulmoides</i> genome. Previous studies have shown that EuFPS homologs can be divided into two groups, namely, FPS 1/3/5 and FPS 2/4, which may play different roles in TPI synthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Suzuki, N. et al. Construction and analysis of EST libraries of the trans-polyisoprene producing plant, Eucommia ulmoides Oliver. Planta 236, 1405â1417 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1826">46</a></sup>. FPS 1/3/5 homologs are involved in long-chain TPI biosynthesis, and FPS 2/4 homologs are in the same group as HbFPS1-3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Wuyun, T. N. et al. The hardy rubber tree genome provides insights into the evolution of polyisoprene biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 11, 429â442 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1830">15</a></sup>. In the newly assembled genome, the evolutionary analysis also indicated that the FPS gene family can be divided into two groups (Figs. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM3">15</a> and S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM3">16</a>), and the expression of FPS1 is more than 200 times higher in leaves and peels with TPI than in xylem and seeds without TPI. This indicates that FPS1 may be a rate-limiting enzyme or at least an important enzyme in TPI synthesis.</p><p>As reported earlier, rubber elongation factor (REF)/small rubber particle protein (SRPP) family proteins are critically important for the biogenesis and stability of rubber particles<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Oh, S. K. et al. Isolation, characterization, and functional analysis of a novel cDNA clone encoding a small rubber particle protein from Hevea brasiliensis. J. Biol. Chem. 274, 17132â17138 (1999)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1845">51</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Lin, T. et al. Genome analysis of Taraxacum kok-saghyz Rodin provides new insights into rubber biosynthesis. Nat. Sci. Rev. 5, 78â87 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1848">52</a></sup>, and there was a positive correlation between REF1 expression and rubber yield in <i>H. brasiliensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Dennis, M. S. &amp; Light, D. R. Rubber elongation factor from Hevea brasiliensis. Identification, characterization, and role in rubber biosynthesis. J. Biol. Chem. 264, 18608â18617 (1989)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1854">53</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Priya, P., Venkatachalam, P. &amp; Thulaseedharan, A. Differential expression pattern of rubber elongation factor (REF) mRNA transcripts from high and low yielding clones of rubber tree (Hevea brasiliensis Muell. Arg.). Plant Cell Rep. 26, 1833â1838 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1857">54</a></sup>. In the <i>E. ulmoides</i> genome (v2.0), as many as nine EuSRPP genes, which is fewer than the 12 <i>H. brasiliensis</i> SRPPs (HbSRPPs), were identified. However, no HbREF1 (138 amino acid) homologs were identified in <i>E. ulmoides</i>, indicating that there are differences in the mechanism of rubber biosynthesis between these two species. Based on the phylogenetic tree (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM3">17</a>), the SRPP family members of <i>E. ulmoides</i> are divided into two clades. The fragments mapped per kilobase of transcript length per million total mapped reads (FPKM) values of SRPP 1 and SRPP 5 in leaves and peels were significantly higher than 300, but there was no significant difference in the expression levels of SRPP5 between TPI tissue and non-TPI tissue. In contrast, the expression levels of SRPP 1 in TPI tissues (leaves and peels) were 7 times higher than those in non-TPI tissues (xylem and seeds). Presumably, SRPP 1 protein, similar to FPS1, may play a more important role in TPI synthesis. Based on the phylogenetic tree of REF/SRPP proteins from rubber-producing and non-rubber-producing plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Tang, C. et al. The rubber tree genome reveals new insights into rubber production and species adaptation. Nat. Plants 2, 16073 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1877">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Wuyun, T. N. et al. The hardy rubber tree genome provides insights into the evolution of polyisoprene biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 11, 429â442 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1880">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Lin, T. et al. Genome analysis of Taraxacum kok-saghyz Rodin provides new insights into rubber biosynthesis. Nat. Sci. Rev. 5, 78â87 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1883">52</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Unver, T. et al. Genome of wild Olive and the evolution of oil biosynthesis. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 114, E9413âE9422 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1886">55</a></sup>, REF/SRPP genes are scattered among some evolutionary branches of non-rubber-producing <i>O. europaea</i>, whereas most REF/SRPP genes in <i>T. kok-saghyz</i>, <i>H. brasiliensis</i> and <i>E. ulmoides</i> are distributed on one major branch (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM3">18</a>). This supports the rubber biosynthesis REF/SRPP gene families originating from multiple sources or from different evolutionary events, as demonstrated in previous studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Tang, C. et al. The rubber tree genome reveals new insights into rubber production and species adaptation. Nat. Plants 2, 16073 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1906">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Wuyun, T. N. et al. The hardy rubber tree genome provides insights into the evolution of polyisoprene biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 11, 429â442 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1909">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Lin, T. et al. Genome analysis of Taraxacum kok-saghyz Rodin provides new insights into rubber biosynthesis. Nat. Sci. Rev. 5, 78â87 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1912">52</a></sup>.</p><p>It is particularly noteworthy that genes within BGCs are involved in the synthesis of rubber initiators. Among the 6 predicted potential terpene secondary metabolite biosynthesis gene clusters, the core of Cluster 15 contains polyisoprene synthase. This gene cluster is 72.5âkb in size and is located at 8,851,940â9,257,031âbp on chromosome 4. The 16 genes in this gene cluster were functionally annotated (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM9">22</a>). Five core protein domains were predicted to be related to secondary metabolism by plantiSMASH<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. Jr. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1922">34</a></sup>, including two encoding polyisoprene synthase (evm.model.Chr4.574 (GPS3) and evm.model.Chr4.575 (GGPS7)), one encoding cytochrome P450 (evm.model.Chr4.579) and two encoding dioxygenase (evm.model.Chr4.588 and evm.model.Chr4.593). In addition, nine members of the SRPP gene family were also present in two locally repeated gene clusters (BGCs): one was composed of three genes (evm.model.000124âf.13, evm.model.000124âf.14 and evm.model.000124âf.15), and the other was composed of four genes (evm.model.chr9.92, evm.model.chr9.94, evm.model.chr9.95, and evm.model.chr9.96), which originated from WGD events or tandem replication. The two remaining members are dispersed repeats on different chromosomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Identification of genes involved in CGA synthesis</h3><p>As a traditional herbal medication, <i>E. ulmoides</i> accumulates CGA, the main phenolic compound, in its leaves and bark<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Wu, L. Q., Zhu, W. X., Zhang, Y. X., Fu, D. D. &amp; Zhang, Z. X. Analysis of chlorogenic acid content and extraction detection method in Eucommia ulmoides Oliv. Food Sci. 26, 187â192 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1937">56</a></sup>. CGAs have long been known to be antioxidants that slow the release of glucose into the bloodstream after a meal<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Ong, K. W., Hsu, A. &amp; Tan, B. K. Chlorogenic acid stimulates glucose transport in skeletal muscle via AMPK activation: a contributor to the beneficial effects of coffee on diabetes. PLoS ONE 7, e32718 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1941">16</a></sup> and reduce blood pressure<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Onakpoya, I. J., Spencer, E. A., Thompson, M. J. &amp; Heneghan, C. J. The effect of chlorogenic acid on blood pressure: a systematic review and meta-analysis of randomized clinical trials. J. Hum. Hypertens. 29, 77â81 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1945">17</a></sup>. In addition, CGAs have antibacterial, antiviral and antitumor activities<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Huang, M. T., Smart, R. C., Wong, C. Q. &amp; Conney, A. H.
