Inhibitory effect of curcumin, chlorogenic acid, caffeic acid, and ferulic acid on tumor promotion in mouse skin by 12-O-tetradecanoylphorbol-13-acetate. Cancer Res. 48, 5941â5946 (1988)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1949">57</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Tajik, N., Tajik, M., Mack, I. &amp; Enck, P. The potential effects of chlorogenic acid, the main phenolic components in coffee, on health: a comprehensive review of the literature. Eur. J. Nutr. 56, 2215â2244 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1952">58</a></sup>. The biosynthesis of CGAs in <i>E. ulmoides</i> occurs through the phenylpropanoid pathway, where the initial reactions are catalyzed by phenylalanine ammonia lyase (PAL), cinnamate 4-hydroxylase (C4H), and 4-coumarin-CoA ligase (4CL) to generate <i>p</i>-coumarin-CoA (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#Fig5">5</a>), which is a precursor for synthesizing CGAs through two different pathways. The first pathway produces <i>p</i>-coumaroyl quinic acid, which is then converted to CGAs. These two reactions are catalyzed by hydroxycinnamoyl-CoA shikimate/quinate hydroxycinnamoyl transferase (HCT) and <i>p</i>-coumaroyl ester 3â²-hydroxylase (C3â²H)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Franke, R. et al. The Arabidopsis REF8 gene encodes the 3-hydroxylase of phenylpropanoid metabolism. Plant J. 30, 33â45 (2002)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1972">59</a></sup>, respectively. The second pathway is <i>p</i>-coumarin-CoA â <i>p</i>-coumaric shikimic acid â caffeoyl shikimic acid â caffeoyl CoA â CGAs, and the four reactions are catalyzed by HCT, C3â²H, HCT and hydroxycinnamoyl-CoA quinate HCT (HQT), respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Niggeweg, R., Michael, A. J. &amp; Martin, C. Engineering plants with increased levels of the antioxidant chlorogenic acid. Nat. Biotechnol. 22, 746â754 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d20745e1983">60</a></sup>. Through homologous gene comparison, 23 candidate genes related to 6 key enzyme gene families involved in the CGA biosynthesis pathway were identified in the <i>E. ulmoides</i> genome, including 7 PAL genes, 8 4CL genes, 2 C4H genes, 2 C3â²H genes, 1 HCT gene, and 3 HQT genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#Fig5">5</a> and Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM4">23</a>). Gene expression profile analysis showed that the expression levels of PAL (PAL2 and PAL5), C3â²H2, C4H6, HQT2 and HCT were significantly higher than the expression levels of other genes. These genes may be key genes whose proteins catalyze CGA biosynthesis in <i>E. ulmoides</i>. In addition, some transcription factors (TFs) have also been reported to regulate CGA biosynthesis, including the <i>MYB</i>, <i>ZIP</i>, and <i>WRKY</i> families<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bartley, G. E. et al. Transcriptional regulation of chlorogenic acid biosynthesis in carrot root slices exposed to UV-B light. Plant Gene 7, 1â10 (2016)." href="#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2009">61</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang, H. et al. Mutation of WRKY transcription factors initiates pith secondary wall formation and increases stem biomass in dicotyledonous plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 22338â22343 (2010)." href="#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2009_1">62</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zha, L. et al. DNA methylation influences chlorogenic acid biosynthesis in Lonicera japonica by mediating LjbZIP8 to regulate phenylalanine ammonia-lyase 2 expression. Front. Plant. Sci. 8, 1178 (2017)." href="#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2009_2">63</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Zhang, J. et al. Genome-wide association studies and expression-based quantitative trait loci analyses reveal roles of HCT2 in caffeoylquinic acid biosynthesis and its regulation by defense-responsive transcription factors in Populus. N. Phytol. 220, 502â516 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2012">64</a></sup>. We identified 1,406 TF-encoding genes in <i>E. ulmoides</i>, representing 5.41% of the total genes. The annotation of these TFs is provided in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM10">S24</a>. We specifically compiled the TFs that control biosynthesis of secondary metabolites (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM11">S25</a>), which are composed primarily of phenolics, terpenoids, glucosinolates, and alkaloids and have been reported to serve the following roles: (1) protect plants against herbivores and pathogens<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Hiruma, K. Roles of plant-derived secondary metabolites during interactions with pathogenic and beneficial microbes under conditions of environmental stress. Microorganisms 7, 362 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2026">65</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Wink, M. Plant secondary metabolites modulate insect behavior-steps toward addiction? Front. Physiol. 9, 364 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2029">66</a></sup> and (2) constitute effective compounds in herbs in traditional medicine<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="McLean, S. &amp; Duncan, A. J. Pharmacological perspectives on the detoxification of plant secondary metabolites: implications for ingestive behavior of herbivores. J. Chem. Ecol. 32, 1213â1228 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2033">67</a></sup>. This information provides valuable information for further research on secondary metabolites in <i>E. ulmoides</i>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="The chlorogenic acid biosynthesis pathway and expression profiles of genes involved in the pathway."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: The chlorogenic acid biosynthesis pathway and expression profiles of genes involved in the pathway.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00406-w/MediaObjects/41438_2020_406_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00406-w/MediaObjects/41438_2020_406_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="615" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>The expression level is presented as log2-transformed fragments mapped per kilobase of transcript length per million total mapped reads (log2-FPKM). L1-L3 leaf; B1-3 bark. The enzymes involved are as follows: PAL phenylalanine ammonia-lyase; C4H cinnamate 4-hydroxylase; 4CL 4-coumaroyl-CoA ligase; HCT hydroxycinnamoyl-CoA: shikimate hydroxycinnamoyl transferase; C3â²H <i>p</i>-coumaroyl ester 3â²-hydroxylase; HQT hydroxycinnamoyl-CoA:quinate hydroxycinnamoyl transferase</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>Haploids play an important role in plant breeding and genome sequencing research. Typically, in vitro anther culture is the most widely used method to induce haploids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="GermanÃ , M. A. Anther culture for haploid and doubled haploid production. Plant Cell Tissue Organ Cult. 104, 283â300 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2072">68</a></sup>. However, anther culture cannot be achieved for the many plant species lacking an established tissue culture system. In this study, an in vivo branch bud heat treatment device<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Kang, X. Y. Heating treatment device for non-separated branches and buds of trees. CN Patent No. 200610113448.X (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2076">69</a></sup> was used for high-temperature treatment of <i>E. ulmoides</i> during the developmental stage of female embryo bud sacs, and three haploid lines were obtained. This shows that high-temperature treatment during embryo sac development is suitable and effective for inducing haploids in <i>E. ulmoides</i>. It also provides a reference for other plant species that do not have an established tissue culture system to induce apomixis at high temperature.</p><p>Various types of genetic and genomic research necessitate an accurate de novo whole-genome assembly as a foundation for developing genetic resources to understand evolution, genetics, inheritance, genomics, epigenetics and functional genomics. However, acquisition of a high-quality genome assembly is an arduous task, especially for species with high heterozygosity (or polymorphism) and/or highly repetitive sequences, including gene duplications, transposons, and short sequence repeats<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Feuillet, C., Leach, J. E., Rogers, J., Schnable, P. S. &amp; Eversole, K. Crop genome sequencing: lessons and rationales. Trends Plant Sci. 16, 77â88 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2089">70</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Yahav, T. &amp; Privman, E. A comparative analysis of methods for de novo assembly of hymenopteran genomes using either haploid or diploid samples. Sci. Rep. 9, 6480 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2092">71</a></sup>. To circumvent these challenges, we specifically developed haploid <i>E. ulmoides</i> plants through parthenogenesis as described above. We used plant material from one of these haploids for genome sequencing, which can enhance the quality of genome assembly and the accuracy of gene annotation owing to the lack of heterozygosity. In addition, the use of advanced PacBio long reads and HiâC data further enhances the quality of the genome. Compared to the initial genome released (v1.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Wuyun, T. N. et al. The hardy rubber tree genome provides insights into the evolution of polyisoprene biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 11, 429â442 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2099">15</a></sup>, the current version (v2.0) has significantly improved assembly quality. For example, the scaffold number was reduced from 29,152 to 501, the scaffold N50 (53.15 MB) increased 28-fold compared to that of v1.0, and the number of gaps between scaffolds decreased from 104,772 in v1.0 to 128 in v2.0 (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM4">26</a>). A previous study in fish showed that the use of a haploid sequence for whole-genome assembly can increase the scaffold N50 by 3- to 10-fold<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Yahav, T. &amp; Privman, E. A comparative analysis of methods for de novo assembly of hymenopteran genomes using either haploid or diploid samples. Sci. Rep. 9, 6480 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2106">71</a></sup>. We thus assume that the 28-fold gain in the scaffold N50 may also be ascribed to the other technologies we used, such as HiâC. In addition, we obtained 520âMb of repetitive sequence, which is 158.24âMb longer than that of the v1.0 genome. Further evaluation with CEGMA revealed 238 (95.97%, completeâ+âpartial) and 230 (92.74%, complete) of 248 core eukaryotic genes in genome v2.0, in contrast with 232 (93.55%, completeâ+âpartial) and 196 (79.03%, complete) in genome v1.0 (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM1">S5</a>). BUSCO analysis identified 93% of the 1,440 single-copy genes in genome v2.0, of which 91.3% were complete copies, which was higher than the 92.8% single-copy genes and 90.0% complete copies identified in genome v1.0 (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM4">S6</a>). Although the 26,001 predicted genes in genome v2.0 is lower than the 26,723 predicted in genome v1.0, the results of the BUSCO and CEGMA evaluations indicate that the v2.0 genome annotation is more accurate and complete.</p><p>The <i>E. ulmoides</i> genome contains a high percentage of repetitive sequences, which poses a great challenge to genome assembly when short reads are used. This is because many short-read assemblers employ an algorithm called de-Bruijn graph representation, which connects short sequence fragments based on their overlapping subsequences. When the de-Bruijn graph is used to extend the assembly, the assembler must deal with repetitive sequences by finding the optimal path from multiple choices<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Yahav, T. &amp; Privman, E. A comparative analysis of methods for de novo assembly of hymenopteran genomes using either haploid or diploid samples. Sci. Rep. 9, 6480 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2123">71</a></sup>. However, this is often impossible, especially when extending contigs through repetitive sequences longer than the read length, resulting in many fragmented assemblies that end in unresolved repetitive sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Simpson, J. T. &amp; Pop, M. The theory and practice of genome sequence assembly. Annu. Rev. Genomics Hum. Genet. 16, 153â172 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2127">72</a></sup>. Therefore, the use of haploid PacBio long reads can significantly reduce the complexity and enhance walk-throughs of repetitive sequences. The majority of repetitive sequences are present in the genome as TEs, which play important roles in genome reorganization and evolution because TE insertions can change genome structure and generate mutations from which new genes can be derived<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Bourque, G. et al. Ten things you should know about transposable elements. Genome Biol. 19, 199 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2131">73</a></sup>. In addition, TEs can modify gene regulatory networks by dispersing vast numbers of promoter and enhancer transcription factor binding sites, insulator sequences, and repressive elements<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Chuong, E. B., Elde, N. C. &amp; Feschotte, C. Regulatory activities of transposable elements: from conflicts to benefits. Nat. Rev. Genet. 18, 71â86 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2135">74</a></sup>. Given its important roles, a genome assembly without accurately assembled repetitive sequences will be deficient for studying genome evolution and gene regulation. The total length of LTRs in the v2.0 genome is ~520âMb, which greatly exceeds the 361.76âMb in genome v1.0. The v2.0 genome contains only 3% noncategorical repeats, which is far less than the 17.16% in the v1.0 genome. Further analysis shows that most LTR-RT expansions occurred in the Pliocene 5.2 Mya. From the perspective of fossil history, the distribution range of <i>E. ulmoides</i> was almost throughout the Northern Hemisphere during the Miocene, and it began to shrink in the Neogene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Momohara, A. Floral and paleoenvironmental history from the late Pliocene to middle Pleistocene in and around central Japan. Palaeogeogr. Palaeoclimatol. Palaeoecol. 108, 281â293 (1994)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2143">75</a></sup>. The reason is that during the Pliocene, the temperature and atmospheric carbon dioxide concentration continued to decline, and in the Pleistocene, Earth entered the glacial period, which changed plant growth and distribution zones<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Cerling, T. E. in C4 Plant Biology (eds Sage, R. F. &amp; Monson, R. K.), 445â469 (Academic Press, 1999)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2147">76</a></sup>. Changes in environmental pressures led to the activation of TEs and plant genome reorganization as well as gene expansion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Grandbastien, M. A. Activation of plant retrotransposons under stress conditions. Trends Plant Sci. 3, 181â187 (1998)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2151">77</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Lisch, D. How important are transposons for plant evolution? Nat. Rev. Genet. 14, 49â61 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2154">78</a></sup>. Half of the resistance gene analogs in this genome were derived from TE insertions (File S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM2">2</a>), suggesting that TE burst might have played an important role in <i>E. ulmoides</i> evolution and stress response.</p><p>The evolutionary history of <i>E. ulmoides</i> is further confirmed to be different from that of genome v1.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Wuyun, T. N. et al. The hardy rubber tree genome provides insights into the evolution of polyisoprene biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 11, 429â442 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2170">15</a></sup>. According to the taxonomy of the Angiosperm Phylogeny Group (APG IV, 2016), <i>E. ulmoides</i> is a latex-producing deciduous species in Garryales, which belongs to lamiids. Wuyun <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Wuyun, T. N. et al. The hardy rubber tree genome provides insights into the evolution of polyisoprene biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 11, 429â442 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2180">15</a></sup> concluded that <i>E. ulmoides</i> is a sister taxon of lamiids and campanulids. In this study, a phylogenetic tree based on genome v2.0 showed that <i>E. ulmoides</i> belongs to lamiids, which is consistent with the systematic classification of angiosperms by The Angiosperm Phylogeny Group APG IV (2016). The inconsistency might arise from the improved genome (2.0) and the selection of more lamiid plants to build phylogenetic trees. WGD or polyploidization has played an important role in plant genome evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Panchy, N., Lehti-Shiu, M. &amp; Shiu, S. H. Evolution of gene duplication in plants. Plant Physiol. 171, 2294â2316 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2191">79</a></sup>. A previous study on genome v1.0 concluded that there was no additional WGD upon the Î³ paleohexaploidization event<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Wuyun, T. N. et al. The hardy rubber tree genome provides insights into the evolution of polyisoprene biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 11, 429â442 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2195">15</a></sup>. However, the present study provides multiple lines of new evidence that the <i>E. ulmoides</i> genome was marked by a lineage-specific paleotetraploidization event superimposed on the earlier paleohexaploidization event shared by all eudicots. By comparing syntenic patterns between <i>E. ulmoides</i> and grape/coffee, we obtained a stable 2:1 synteny ratio (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#Fig3">3f</a> and Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM4">S18</a>). These results provide a basis for future comparative genomics analyses of asterid evolution.</p><p>The IPP derived from the MVA and MEP pathways is considered a substrate for synthetic TPI<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Bamba, T. et al. Contribution of mevalonate and methylerythritol phosphate pathways to polyisoprenoid biosynthesis in the rubber-producing plant Eucommia ulmoides Oliver. Z. Naturforsch. C. J. Biosci. 65, 363â372 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2216">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Vranova, E., Coman, D. &amp; Gruissem, W. Structure and dynamics of the isoprenoid pathway network. Mol. Plant 5, 318â333 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2219">80</a></sup>. <sup>13</sup>C isotopic labeling showed that TPI was obtained from both the MVA and MEP pathways in <i>E. ulmoides</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Bamba, T. et al. Contribution of mevalonate and methylerythritol phosphate pathways to polyisoprenoid biosynthesis in the rubber-producing plant Eucommia ulmoides Oliver. Z. Naturforsch. C. J. Biosci. 65, 363â372 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2227">45</a></sup>. Studies in <i>H. brasiliensis</i> and dandelion-type plants have shown that the MVA pathway is mainly involved in the synthesis of <i>cis</i>-rubbers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Tang, C. et al. The rubber tree genome reveals new insights into rubber production and species adaptation. Nat. Plants 2, 16073 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2238">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Lin, T. et al. Genome analysis of Taraxacum kok-saghyz Rodin provides new insights into rubber biosynthesis. Nat. Sci. Rev. 5, 78â87 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2241">52</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Vranova, E., Coman, D. &amp; Gruissem, W. Structure and dynamics of the isoprenoid pathway network. Mol. Plant 5, 318â333 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2244">80</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Putter, K. M., Van Deenen, N., Unland, K., Prufer, D. &amp; Schulze Gronover, C. Isoprenoid biosynthesis in dandelion latex is enhanced by the overexpression of three key enzymes involved in the mevalonate pathway. BMC Plant Biol. 17, 88 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2247">81</a></sup>. Previous research using the v1.0 genome showed that <i>Eucommia</i> is similar to <i>H. brasiliensis</i> and <i>T. kok-saghyz</i>, and the main route of IPP synthesis is the MVA pathway<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Wuyun, T. N. et al. The hardy rubber tree genome provides insights into the evolution of polyisoprene biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 11, 429â442 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2260">15</a></sup>. However, a different result was obtained in this study. At least one enzyme in each process of the MEP pathway in <i>E. ulmoides</i> was predominantly expressed in the TPI-containing leaves and central peel. Therefore, the MEP pathway, rather than the MVA pathway, plays a major role in the synthesis of IPP for TPI biosynthesis. Pilot studies conducted previously produced similar results (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM8">21</a> and Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM3">14</a>). Such a discrepancy from the results of Wuyun <i>et al</i> may result from variation in the tree population. It is possible that the trees we studied are from different provenances and have adapted to different ecotypes or climate types. This variation may provide an opportunity for selective breeding for the improvement of TPI synthesis.</p><p>To date, four protein families related to long-chain CPI rubber elongation have been characterized: <i>cis</i>-prenyltransferases (CPTs), SRPP, REF and Nogo-B receptor (NgBR)-like protein<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Oh, S. K. et al. Isolation, characterization, and functional analysis of a novel cDNA clone encoding a small rubber particle protein from Hevea brasiliensis. J. Biol. Chem. 274, 17132â17138 (1999)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2283">51</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Dennis, M. S. &amp; Light, D. R. Rubber elongation factor from Hevea brasiliensis. Identification, characterization, and role in rubber biosynthesis. J. Biol. Chem. 264, 18608â18617 (1989)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2286">53</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Koyama, T. Molecular analysis of prenyl chain elongating enzymes. Biosci. Biotechnol. Biochem. 63, 1671â1676 (1999)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2289">82</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Epping, J. et al. A rubber transferase activator is necessary for natural rubber biosynthesis in dandelion. Nat. Plants 1, 15048 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2292">83</a></sup>. CPT is responsible for adding IPP to the substrate in the <i>cis</i>-configuration to form long-chain CPI<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Koyama, T. Molecular analysis of prenyl chain elongating enzymes. Biosci. Biotechnol. Biochem. 63, 1671â1676 (1999)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2299">82</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Takahashi, S. &amp; Koyama, T. Structure and function of cis-prenyl chain elongating enzymes. Chem. Rec. 6, 194â205 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2302">84</a></sup>. REF/SRPP is involved in the stabilization of the rubber particle membrane<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Schmidt, T. et al. Characterization of rubber particles and rubber chain elongation in Taraxacum kok-saghyz. BMC Biochem. 11, 11 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2306">85</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Hillebrand, A. et al. Down-regulation of small rubber particle protein expression affects integrity of rubber particles and rubber content in Taraxacum brevicorniculatum. PLoS ONE 7, e41874 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2309">86</a></sup>. NgBR-like protein is a homolog of the human Nogo-B receptor and has been demonstrated to be essential for rubber biosynthesis in dandelion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Epping, J. et al. A rubber transferase activator is necessary for natural rubber biosynthesis in dandelion. Nat. Plants 1, 15048 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2314">83</a></sup>. The NgBR-like protein functions as a mediating protein between REF and CPT to form the ternary complex to increase CPT activity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Yamashita, S. et al. Identification and reconstitution of the rubber biosynthetic machinery on rubber particles from Hevea brasiliensis. Elife 5, e19022 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2318">87</a></sup>. However, the details of long-chain TPI biosynthesis after IPP formation in <i>E. ulmoides</i> via the MVA and MEP pathways remain largely unknown. Wuyun <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Wuyun, T. N. et al. The hardy rubber tree genome provides insights into the evolution of polyisoprene biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 11, 429â442 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2328">15</a></sup> found that some key amino acids in the conserved features/sites of FPS 1, 3, and 5 differed from those of FPS 2 and 4 and all three <i>HbFPS1-3</i>. At the same time, long-chain TPI was obtained after overexpression of FPS5 in tobacco, indicating that FPS 1, 3, and 5 may participate in the synthesis of long-chain TPI. Wuyun <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Wuyun, T. N. et al. The hardy rubber tree genome provides insights into the evolution of polyisoprene biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 11, 429â442 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2339">15</a></sup> also found that the expression levels of EuSRPP1, 2, and 7 instead of REFs corresponded to the accumulation of Eu-rubber. The expression levels of three REF genes were almost all zero in expression in all tissues examined<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Wuyun, T. N. et al. The hardy rubber tree genome provides insights into the evolution of polyisoprene biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 11, 429â442 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2343">15</a></sup>. In our study, we failed to annotate FPS5 in the first round of gene discovery because of its low expression levels in most tissues (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM8">21</a>), indicating the possibility of increasing TPI synthesis by overexpressing FPS1. In the v2.0 genome, only 9 SRPP gene family members were detected, and no genes that are homologous to HbREF1 (138 amino acids) were identified, indicating that they may be pseudogenes with incomplete gene sequences arising from the fragmentation of the contig/scaffold sequences in the v1.0 genome. In addition, 3 CPT genes and an NgBR homologous gene were found in the v2.0 genome, and their expression levels were much lower than those of FPS1 and SRPP1 (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM8">21</a>). This suggests that although CPT, REF, and NgBR-like proteins were reported to participate in long-chain CPI biosynthesis, whether they are functionally involved in the biosynthesis of long-chain TPI is unclear and needs to be further investigated. Given that the rubber in <i>E. ulmoides</i> is pure TPI<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bamba, T., Fukusaki, E., Nakazawa, Y. &amp; Kobayashi, A. In-situ chemical analyses of trans-polyisoprene by histochemical staining and Fourier transform infrared microspectroscopy in a rubber-producing plant, Eucommia ulmoides Oliver. Planta 215, 934â939 (2002)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2357">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Tangpakdee, J. et al. Structure and biosynthesis of trans-polyisoprene from Eucommia ulmoides. Phytochemistry 45, 75â80 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2360">88</a></sup>, it is still not possible to sketch the biochemical scenario for long-chain TPI synthesis in <i>E. ulmoides</i> with only the genes whose counterparts have been reported in other species as being involved in CPI synthesis. Clearly, more functional genes and enzymes need to be identified and characterized to delineate the underlying biochemical reactions and pathways of TPI synthesis. Corresponding to CPT, there should be at least one <i>trans</i>-prenyltransferase (TPT) enzyme that can add IPP in a <i>trans</i> configuration during the synthesis of long-chain TPI in <i>E. ulmoides</i>. TPT is a large gene family. Its main function is to add IPP to the precursor in the <i>trans</i> configuration. According to the chain length of the product, it can be divided into short-chain and long-chain TPTs, where short-chain TPTs are widely found in plants, mainly including geranyl GPS, FPS, and GGPS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Tang, C. et al. The rubber tree genome reveals new insights into rubber production and species adaptation. Nat. Plants 2, 16073 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2380">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Lin, T. et al. Genome analysis of Taraxacum kok-saghyz Rodin provides new insights into rubber biosynthesis. Nat. Sci. Rev. 5, 78â87 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2383">52</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Liang, P. H., Ko, T. P. &amp; Wang, A. H. J. Structure, mechanism and function of prenyltransferases. Eur. J. Biochem. 269, 3339â3354 (2002)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2386">89</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Liu, J. et al. The Chromosome-based rubber tree genome provides new insights into spurge genome evolution and rubber biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 13, 336â350 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2389">90</a></sup>. However, the long-chain TPT responsible for adding IPP to precursors for long-chain TPI rubber elongation is still unknown. Therefore, in addition to identifying long-chain TPTs, the functions of FPS and SRPP in Eu-rubber synthesis also need to be further characterized because existing evidence indicates that they may play a role during the synthesis of long-chain TPI<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Wuyun, T. N. et al. The hardy rubber tree genome provides insights into the evolution of polyisoprene biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 11, 429â442 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2393">15</a></sup>. Such divergence among species implies that the biosynthesis of polyisoprene in angiosperms is polyphyletic in origin.</p><p>In conclusion, the haploid <i>E. ulmoides</i> genome sequencing project has provided a highly accurate and contiguous reference genome sequence for evolutionary and genomic studies. Our preliminary analyses have added valuable information to the genomic resources of lamiids and led to novel conclusions regarding its evolution in terms of WGD, BGCs and rubber synthesis pathways. We believe that this work will be instrumental not only for accelerating evolutionary, genetic and genomic studies in <i>E. ulmoides</i> but also for advancing our understanding of the molecular mechanisms underlying the biosynthesis and regulation of rubber and chlorogenic acid as well as other valuable secondary metabolites such as rutin and quercetin.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Induction of haploid by high-temperature exposure</h3><p>The female flower buds of <i>E. ulmoides</i> in the developmental stage of embryo sac formation were treated at either 48âÂ°C or 54âÂ°C for 4â6âh, and untreated female flower buds were used as controls. Seeds were harvested and planted in a room at 25âÂ°C for germination. Karyotyping was conducted with the aid of flow cytometry and anatomical sections to identify authentic <i>Eucommia</i> haploid plants.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec12">Plant material</h3><p>Fresh leaves of haploid <i>E. ulmoides</i> plants originally induced by parthenogenesis were grown in a greenhouse for genome sequencing and HiâC analysis. Apical buds, young leaves, mature leaves, old leaves, bark, roots, peels, seeds, lateral buds, male flowers (SRX2447963, SRX2447934, SRX2447933, and SRX1160206), female flowers (SRX1160207), and stem tissue of <i>E. ulmoides</i> were harvested, immediately frozen in liquid nitrogen and stored at â80âÂ°C for RNA-seq experiments. The resulting RNA-seq data were deposited into the Sequence Read Archive (SRA) (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra</a>) public repository.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">SMRT long-read sequencing</h3><p>The DNeasy Plant Mini Kit (TIANGEN, Beijing, China) was used to extract high-quality genomic DNA from young leaves of haploid <i>E. ulmoides</i>. For PacBio sequencing, a single-molecule real-time (SMRT) bell library with a 40-kb insert fragment length was constructed according to the manufacturerâs protocol (PacBio, CA).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">Illumina short-read sequencing</h3><p>For Illumina (San Diego, CA) sequencing, 350-bp libraries with an average insert length of 350âbp were prepared using the NEBNext Ultra DNA Library Prep Kit and sequenced using the Illumina HiSeq X Ten platform at the Novogene Bioinformatics Institute, Beijing.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec15">HiâC library construction and sequencing</h3><p>A HiâC library was prepared using the Dovetail<sup>TM</sup> HiâC Library Preparation Kit. Nuclear chromatin was fixed with formaldehyde. Fixed chromatin was digested with DpnII, and the sticky ends were filled with biotinylated nucleotides at the digested DNA ends prior to ligation. The chromatin was then reverse-crosslinked to free DNA from proteins, and the purified DNA was processed to remove any free biotin from the linked fragments. The DNA was then cut into 350-bp fragments, and the biotinylated fragments were enriched by pulling down with <i>streptavidin</i>-coated magnetic <i>beads</i>, followed by PCR amplification to generate a library. The size of the inserted fragment was checked using a Qubit 2.0 fluorometer and an Agilent 2100 fluorometer. The effective concentrations of the libraries were accurately quantified by Q-PCR to confirm their quality, and the libraries were sequenced on the Illumina NovaSeq platform.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec16">Genome assembly</h3><p>Before assembling, the âdalignerâ option in the FALCON assembler was used to correct the errors in PacBio long reads using PacBio short reads less than 6000âbp in length to generate consensus sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Pendleton, M. et al. Assembly and diploid architecture of an individual human genome via single-molecule technologies. Nat. Methods 12, 780â786 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2489">91</a></sup>. Then, FALCON was used to identify the overlaps between all pairs of preassembled error-corrected reads. The read overlaps were used to construct a directed string graph following Myersâ algorithm. Contigs were constructed by finding the paths from the string graph (falcon_sense_optionâ=â- output_multi -min_idt 0.70 -min_cov 4 -max_n_read 300 -n_core 12; overlap_filtering_settingâ=â-max_diff 100 -max_cov 100 -min_cov 4 -n_core 12). Error correction of the preceding assembly was performed by the consensusâcalling algorithm Quiver with PacBio reads<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Nonhybrid, finished microbial genome assemblies from long-read SMRT sequencing data. Nat. Methods 10, 563â569 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2493">21</a></sup>. The Illumina reads were also used to correct the contigs with Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2497">92</a></sup>. The heterozygosity in the error-corrected contigs was then removed by purge_haplotigs with Illumina clean reads.</p><p>The HiâC clean data were aligned to the preceding assembly using BWA software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754â1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2504">22</a></sup>. SAMTOOLS was used to remove duplicates (parameters: rmdup) and non-aligned data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078â2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2508">23</a></sup>, and only the read pairs with both reads in the pair aligned to contigs were considered for scaffolding. The physical coverage of each read pair was defined as the total bases spanned by the sequences of the reads and the gap between the two reads when mapped to contigs. The per base physical coverage of each base in the contig was defined as the number of read pairs that physically included it. Given this, misassembly was detected by a sudden drop in the per-base physical coverage in a contig. According to the physical coverage of the alignment result, misassemblies were sheared to correct errors by SALSA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Ghurye, J., Pop, M., Koren, S., Bickhart, D. &amp; Chin, C. S. Scaffolding of long read assemblies using long range contact information. BMC Genom. 18, 527 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2512">93</a></sup>. Based on the linkage information and restriction enzyme sites, the string graph formulation was used to construct the scaffold graph with LACHESIS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119â1125 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2516">24</a></sup>. Finally, the scaffolds were anchored to 17 chromosomes, and then the interaction matrix heat map of all chromosomes was visualized with a resolution of 500âkb.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec17">Evaluation of the genome assembly</h3><p>To evaluate the accuracy and completeness of the assembled <i>Eucommia</i> genome, Burrows-Wheeler Aligner (BWA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754â1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2531">22</a></sup> software was used to compare a small-fragment library with an average insert length of 350âbp to the assembled genome. The alignment rate, extent of genome coverage, and depth distribution were used to assess assembly integrity and sequencing uniformity. CEGMA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061â1067 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2535">25</a></sup> and BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d20745e2539">26</a></sup> were used to assess the integrity of the final genome assembly.</p><p>Additional detailed methods, including annotation of repetitive sequences, gene prediction, phylogenetic tree reconstruction, species divergence time estimation, expansion and contraction of gene families, genome synteny and WGD assessment, transcriptome sequencing, and analysis, identification of resistance genes, identification of CGA synthesis-related genes, identification of transcription factors and phylogenetic analysis, are available in File S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00406-w#MOESM2">2</a>.</p></div></div></section>

              <p>All sequencing reads and the genome assembly have been deposited in the NCBI database under accession number PRJNA599775.</p>
